c) Blending chart based on Performance Grade (PG) 
The blending chart is used to find out the rate of the virgin asphalt binder / recycling 
agent added to the recycled mixture to reach the required performance of the project. 
The value of G*/sinδ is determined for the RAP binder and the virgin asphalt binder, and 
these values are used in the blending chart (Fig. C-1). To illustrate this, the following 
example will be used: 
Suppose that the required grade of the recycled mixture for the project is PG76-10 and 
the performance grade of the virgin asphalt binder is PG64-10, and it is required to 
determine the required percentage of the virgin asphalt binder to be added. 
 Determine the value of G*/sinδ of RAP binder at a temperature of 76 degrees 
Celsius and plot the value on the left vertical axis, which represents 100% of the 
RAP binder (point A) and suppose that the value is 80 kPa. 
 Determine the value of G*/sinδ of the virgin asphalt binder at a temperature of 
76°C and plot the value on the right vertical axis, which represents 100% of 
virgin asphalt binder (point C) and suppose that the value is 0.3 kPa. 
 Draw a line between data points A and C, the point of intersection with the 
stiffness line at 2 kPa is point B and the point of intersection with the stiffness 
line at 1 kPa is point D. 
 The coordinates corresponding to point B and point D on the horizontal axis are 
the percentage of the virgin asphalt binder that will be added to the recycled 
mixture, and it represents here a rate of 63% to 77%.

 Draw point A, which represents the viscosity of RAP binder 80,000 poises, on the 
left vertical axis, which represents 100% of the RAP binder. 
 Draw point C, which represents the viscosity of the recycling agent 100 poises, 
on the right vertical axis, which represents 100% of the recycling agent. 
 Draw a horizontal line corresponding to the target viscosity of 2000 poises. 
 Draw a line between data points A and C so that it intersects with the horizontal 
line of the target viscosity at point B. 
  The coordinates corresponding to point B on the horizontal axis is the 
percentage of the recycling agent that will be added to the recycled mixture, and 
it represents here 54%.

b) Blending chart based on viscosity 
The blending chart is used either to find out the percentage of the virgin asphalt 
binder/recycling agent added to the recycled mixture, if the viscosity of the RAP binder, 
the virgin asphalt binder/recycling agent and the target viscosity of the recycled 
mixture is known, or it is used to determine the viscosity of the virgin asphalt binder/ 
recycling agent, if its percentage in the mix is known, the viscosity of both the RAP 
binder and the target viscosity of the recycled mixture. Viscosity is determined 
according to ASTM D2171. 
To understand the use of the blending chart (Fig. B-1), the following example will be 
used: 
It was decided to use 100% of reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) in one of the projects 
for hot in-place recycling. The viscosity of the RAP binder is 80,000 poises, the target 
viscosity of the recycled mix is 2,000 poises, and the available recycling agent has a 
viscosity of 100 poises. It is required to determine the required percentage of the 
recycling agent.

Appendix A: Blending Charts 
For low content of RAP (≤ 15% by total HMA weight), no RAP characterization is needed. 
For high percentages of RAP over 15%, you will need to extract and recover the RAP 
binder and determine its properties to construct a blending chart. The RAP binder has 
to be extracted and then recovered for testing. A solvent extraction must be used when 
recovering the RAP binder. 
 
a) Superpave and Marshall System 
The physical properties of the recovered RAP binder and the desired final binder grade 
are needed for constructing of the blending chart. The extracted and recovered RAP 
binder must be tested in the dynamic shear rheometer (DSR) at a high temperature as 
if it was aged in the rolling thin film oven (RTFO). Based on early results Figures A-1 
and A-2 were developed for RAP content versus PMB high temperatures for recovered 
binder grade of PG≤ 82 and PG > 82 oC, respectively. According to RAP content (% by 
total HMA weight), those figures may be used as guidance to evaluate the minimum 
virgin PMB grade required to achieve (after mixing with RAP) the project target PG 
grade.

 Quality Assurance Procedures 
4.5.1 Compaction and Thickness 
The nuclear method can be used to determine the compacted material's field density in 
direct transmission mode as per AASHTO T355 or the sand cone method according to 
AASHTO T191 can be used. The average compaction percentage for five measurements 
must be at least 98% of the maximum dry density obtained from the modified Proctor 
test with no individual test less than 95%. Less compaction with depth may be specified 
if the compacted lift thickness is relatively thick, more than 300 mm. 
The thickness of the compacted reclaimed material is checked every 100 m using depth 
probe measurement. 
  
4.5.2 Reclaimed Material Gradation  
It is recommended that the reclaimed materials meet the gradation requirements listed 
in Table No. (6). Samples must be taken to ensure the maximum size requirements for 
the reclaimed materials, so that the percentage passing through a specific sieve is not 
less than 100%. 
Table (6) Gradation Requirements for FDR

4.5   Quality Assurance Procedures 
4.  Full Depth Reclamation 
  
  
 
  
Recycling Asphalt Pavement 
within Road Projects 
Kingdom of Saudi Arabia 
Roads general Authority

 صفحة 71 
 من 77
 
recommended optimal moisture at the beginning of compaction. Obviously, the correct 
moisture content is critical in achieving proper compaction with minimal effort. Due to 
the time delay between material shaping, initial compaction, and final compaction, a 
light amount of water is usually sprayed on the surface of the reclaimed material before 
final compaction is performed. 
 
4.4.2.3 Curing 
Reclaimed materials need to be properly cured by allowing excess moisture required 
during the compaction process to escape in order to achieve maximum strength and 
prevent raveling before applying the surface layer. No criteria have been established 
to determine when adequate curing has been performed due to the wide variety of 
reclaimed materials and stabilization agents. When using chemical stabilizing agents, 
the appropriate surface moisture must be maintained for the materials to gain strength. 
This usually takes 2 to 7 days. 
 
 
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
4.4   Construction and Execution – Execution 
4.  Full Depth Reclamation 
  
  
 
  
Recycling Asphalt Pavement 
within Road Projects 
Kingdom of Saudi Arabia 
Roads general Authority 
General Department of Quality 
& Environment
 صفحة 70 
 من 77
 
4.4.2 Execution 
Prior to the beginning of full depth reclamation process, clean debris and dirt, all 
foreign materials, thermoplastic road markings, cat eyes, crack fillers and other 
similar materials must be properly removed from the road as required. 
 
4.4.2.1 Pulverization and Mixing 
A reclaimer with capability to pulverize and mix a minimum of 300 mm of asphalt 
pavement and underlying materials has to be used to obtain a homogeneous mixture of 
the desired gradation Joints between successive pulverization passes along the 
longitudinal direction should overlap by at least 15 cm and transverse joints should 
overlap by at least 60 cm. Dry stabilizing materials can be spread on the road surface 
before pulverizing. Bituminous materials and water are added during the pulverizing 
and mixing process to the reclaimed materials in the required proportions. Water can 
be added using tank trucks or through the additive system on board the reclaimer, 
while bituminous materials are added through the additive system on board the 
reclaimer. It is important to periodically check the depth and gradation of the reclaimed 
material to ensure it matches the mix design. The maximum particle size check is 
usually done more often than determining the overall gradation of the reclaimed 
material. 
 
4.4.2.2 Compaction 
The degree of compaction achieved is one of the primary determinants of the future 
performance of the reclaimed mix and poor compaction leads to problems such as 
rutting and raveling. Usually more than one compactor is needed to densify the 
reclaimed material. The characteristics of the reclaimed mix such as depth and degree 
of compaction will determine the type of compactors should be used. A field test strip 
has to be used to establish the best combination of rollers for best degree of 
compaction. Moisture contents of all processed materials must be within 2 % of the

4.4.1.3 Calibrated Bulk Spreaders 
Must ensure consistent, precise and 
uniform distribution of dry stabilizers while 
keeping dust to a minimum. Care must be 
taken so that an excessive dust cloud does 
not arise during application and mixing of 
dry setting agents. Dry stabilizers are more 
susceptible to environmental influences such as wind and rain. 
 
4.4.1.4 Tanker Trucks 
Tanker trucks are used to deliver water and 
bituminous stabilizing agents (emulsified 
asphalt and foamed asphalt) to the project 
site to feed the reclaimer using the 
electronic liquid addition system on-board. 
  
 
4.4.1.5 Rollers  
Vibratory padfoot, vibratory smooth drum, 
pneumatic, and static steel rollers can be 
used for compaction. Due to the thickness 
and properties of reclaimed materials, the 
rollers used are usually heavy and large. A 
vibratory drum roller, a pneumatic roller, 
or a padfoot roller can be used for initial compaction. Intermediate compaction is 
usually performed using a pneumatic roller, while final compaction is performed 
using a vibratory or non- vibratory drum roller.

 Construction and execution 
4.4.1 Equipment 
The process of recycling asphalt pavement layers and the underlying materials (FDR) 
does not require a lot of equipment, and the minimum equipment required is to use a 
modern, self-propelled equipment for pulverizing and mixing reclaimed materials 
(reclaimer) equipped with a system for adding liquid asphalt materials, a grader and 
one or more compactors. Some Projects require the addition of additives, so the use of 
a water and emulsified asphalt truck and calibrated bulk spreaders are required. 
Materials should not be mixed or placed during rainfall, dust or sand storms and when 
the ambient temperature is less than 5 °C. 
 
4.4.1.1 Reclaimer 
The reclaimer should have the capacity to 
pulverize and mix a minimum of 300 mm of 
material. The cutting drum must be able to 
rotate at a number of different speeds for 
pulverizing and mixing a variety of material 
thicknesses and types. The orientation of 
the cutting tools is usually in a chevron pattern which enhances mixing and 
results in the material being moved to the center of the cutting drum as opposed 
to the helical pattern found in conventional milling machines. The equipment must 
have an electronic liquid addition system, a meter to record the flow and the total 
amount of liquids added, and a system to control the rate of adding liquid. 
4.4.1.2 Motor Grader 
Used For pre-shaping, aerating, spreading 
and final shaping of the reclaimed material.

not more than 48 hours. The constant weight of a sample is defined as 0.05% 
change in weight or less in 2 hours. After removing the samples from the oven, 
the samples are left to cool at ambient temperature for a period of not less than 
12 hours and not more than 24 hours. 
8. After completion of curing, the specific gravity of the compacted asphalt mix 
(Gmb) is calculated according to AASHTO T 166. 
9. Perform moisture conditioning on three compacted samples at each emulsified 
asphalt content by applying a vacuum of 13 to 67 kPa absolute pressures for a 
time duration required to vacuum saturate samples to 55 – 75% percent.  
10. For indirect tensile strength, the dry and cured set is placed in a sealing bag 
and immersed in a water bath at 25 ± 1°C for 2 hours prior to testing and the 
wet moisture conditioned set is placed in a water bath at 25 ± 1°C for 24 hours 
and then tested Immediately after removal from the water bath in accordance 
with AASHTO T 283.

samples are prepared at either the highest emulsified asphalt content in the 
design or the highest and lowest emulsified asphalt contents in the design.

samples. In the first case of samples at the highest emulsion content and after

percent passing 0.075 mm exceeds 20 percent, or sand equivalent values are 
lower than 30 and/or plasticity index values are larger than 6. 
4. A modified Proctor test shall be carried out according to AASHTO T180 (method 
D) to determine the optimum moisture content to obtain the maximum dry 
density by mixing the reclaimed materials with at least four trial mixing water 
contents, typically in 1 percent increments. This optimum moisture content is 
the total fluid content (water plus emulsified asphalt) for the materials. 
5. Mix reclaimed materials at optimum moisture content with a minimum of four 
different proportions of emulsified asphalt contents in 0.5 to 1.0 percent 
increments covering a range typically between 1 to 4 percent, or greater, by 
dry weight of reclaimed materials. It should be noted that the optimum 
moisture content must be maintained at the different percentages of the 
emulsified asphalt constant, in other words, if the optimum moisture content 
obtained from the modified Proctor test is 5% and the percentage of emulsified 
asphalt added is 2%, then the percentage of mixing water added is 3%. The 
reclaimed materials must be mixed with dry additives first at ambient 
temperature (25±5 °C), then finally emulsified asphalt is added and the mixing 
is continued. One sample should be mixed at a time, and the mixing time must 
not exceed 60 seconds. For mixtures with a sand equivalent value > 30, use 45 
to 65 percent of the total liquids content determined earlier. For mixtures with 
a sand equivalent value ≤ 30, use 60 to 75 percent of the total liquids content. 
6. After mixing, samples cured in plastic containers at a temperature of 40±2°C 
for 30±3 minutes. After curing samples are compacted by using a gyratory 
compactor according to AASHTO T180 at a number of gyrations equal to 30 to 
produce a sample with a height of 75 ± 5 mm and a diameter of 150 mm. A total 
of 6 samples should be prepared for each content of emulsified asphalt for the 
indirect tensile strength test and divided into two sets, three of these samples 
are called the dry set and placed in the air, while the other three samples are 
called the wet set and placed in a bath Water at a certain temperature for a 
certain period of time. Average percentage of air voids for each group should 
be approximately equal. 
7. After compaction, remove the samples from the mold and place them in an 
oven equipped with ventilation outlets for curing at a temperature of 60 ± 1 °C 
to obtain a constant sample weight for a period of not less than 16 hours and

Obtaining the optimum content of emulsified asphalt can be summarized as follows: 
1. Obtain samples from the existing asphalt pavement, and underlying materials 
to the planned recycling depth by coring, test pits, or milling of the areas to be 
recycled. Crush the samples at ambient to obtain reclaimed asphalt pavement 
(RAP) for mix design. Use a jaw crusher, laboratory milling machine or other 
suitable method to model the shape and gradation expected during reclaiming. 
Samples of RAP shall be sieved per AASHTO T27, with the exception that drying 
the RAP to a constant mass shall be performed at 40 ± 2 °C. A washed sieve 
analysis per AASHTO T11 shall be conducted on a representative sample in the 
presence of a significant amount of fine particles. RAP materials should be 
processed to model the gradation expected during recycling or combine RAP 
materials to produce a gradation within the limits of Table 4. 
2. Combine the RAP, batched to the gradation in previous step, with the underlying 
material to the proportion found in the field. Combined material should be dried 
at 40 ± 2 °C and wash sieved as per AASHTO T11 and T27. 
3.  Determine the sand equivalent, liquid limit and plastic limit and plasticity index 
of the reclaimed materials, according to AASHTO T176, T89 and T90, 
respectively. Additives, such as cement or lime, may be necessary if the

 Design of Full Depth reclamation Mix 
4.3.1 Design steps 
Although there is no globally accepted design guide for full depth reclamation mix 
design, guidelines have been developed by several road agencies based on laboratory 
tests, empirical formulas, or previous experience on similar projects. The design 
steps can be summarized as follows:  
1. Obtaining samples from the full thickness of the asphalt pavement and a 
predetermined portion of the underlying materials to the depth to be recycled 
by taking core samples, test pits or milling of the areas to be recycled. 
2. Crushing the asphalt pavement to obtain reclaimed asphalt pavement material. 
3. Combining the RAP, with the underlying material to the proportion found in the 
field. Perform sieve analysis, sand equivalent and plasticity index of the 
reclaimed materials.  
4. Determining the need and selection of the stabilizing agent and additives, if 
necessary.  
5. Determining the optimum moisture content to obtain the maximum dry density 
of the reclaimed material. 
6. Determining the optimum content of emulsified asphalt or foamed asphalt to 
achieve the design criteria of the mixture such as indirect tensile strength 
(superpave) or Marshall stability. 
7. Determining the job mix formula (JMF). Once all laboratory tests of the 
materials are completed, the information is available to create a job Mix 
Formula (JMF).   
 
4.3.2 Design using emulsified asphalt 
The design process using emulsified asphalt is based on determining the optimal 
content for coating the reclaimed materials and any additives to obtain a homogeneous 
mixture with appropriate durability that meets the requirements of the special mixture. 
The optimal emulsified asphalt content is defined as the content that meets the mixture 
requirements specified in Table 5. 
 
  
4.3    Design of Full Depth Reclamation Mix

4.2.5 Recycling Agent 
The most common recycling agents used in the cold recycling process are the 
emulsified recycling agents. The recycling agent must comply with ASTM D5505. 
  
  
4.2.6 Water 
Water can be added to reclaimed materials to achieve a homogeneous easily 
compacted mixture. Water should be free of any high concentrations of acids, 
alkalis, salts, sugars and other organic materials, chemicals or harmful 
substances. If the water quality is questionable, it should be tested according to 
ASTM C1602 or to the specifications of the Ministry of Transport and logistic 
services. The water used to produce the foamed asphalt must be pure and not 
contain sediment that would impede the flow through the nozzles in the recycling 
unit. 
  
4.2.7 Chemical Additives (Optional) 
 There are several types of mineral or chemical additives that have proven useful 
in improving the performance of reclaimed materials, such as fly ash, Portland 
cement, and lime. The primary function of these stabilizing agents is to increase 
the strength of the reclaimed material by holding the particles together and acting 
as a catalyst to increase the strength of the materials, allowing the road to be 
opened to traffic sooner. Chemical additives are added in small amounts, typically 
0.5 to 1.5% by weight of the reclaimed materials. 
• cement 
Cement may be added as specified in the mix design and to comply with 
specifications for type 1 or type 2 cement (AASHTO M 85, ASHTO M 240 or ASTM 
C150, ASTM C595). Typically, the use of Portland cement is limited to reclaimed 
materials with a plasticity index of less than 10%. If the plasticity index of the 
reclaimed material is more than 10%, it is preferable to use lime.

Chemical Additives

4.2.2 Aggregate 
Although it is not often necessary to add aggregate, some projects may require 
adding new aggregates in order to adjust the gradation, improve the stability and 
durability. For example, if the reclaimed material is too coarse to be compacted 
properly, the aggregate added should be of finer materials in order to produce a 
more homogeneous mix. Conversely, if the reclaimed material is very fine or has 
a high plasticity index, there will be a need to add materials with a coarser 
gradation. The new aggregate must be evaluated before use to ensure that it 
conforms to the specifications in terms of gradation and physical properties.  
4.2.3 Emulsified Asphalt 
Emulsified asphalt is the most common material for use with reclaimed materials 
and the main reason for its use is the ability to mix it well with cold reclaimed 
materials and obtain a mixture of reclaimed materials coated with bitumen. The 
chemical reaction between the emulsified asphalt and reclaimed materials has a 
significant impact on the stability of the emulsified asphalt, so it is important to 
ensure the compatibility of the emulsified asphalt with the reclaimed materials 
during mix design. For cationic emulsified asphalt, use CSS-1 or CSS-1h 
conforming to AASHTO M208. For anionic emulsified asphalt, use HFMS-2, HFMS-
2h, or HFM S-2s conforming to AASHTO M140 
4.2.4 Foamed Asphalt 
Foamed asphalt is produced by mixing hot asphalt binder with small amounts of 
cold water. When cold water comes into contact with hot bitumen, it turns into 
steam, which becomes trapped in the form of bubbles on the asphalt. The result 
is that the hot asphalt binder expands and turns into a thin layer asphalt foam that 
has a high covering capacity that is about 10 times more than that of a 
conventional asphalt binder. The foam condition lasts only for a few minutes, after 
which the asphalt binder returns to its original properties and this system 
requires a special chamber to form the foamed asphalt. The foamy state of the 
bitumen allows it to disperse and cover the reclaimed material well.

 Chemical stabilization: Chemical stabilization is achieved by adding chemical 
additives such as cement (Portland or hydraulic), lime (hydrated lime or 
quicklime), Class C fly ash, Class F fly ash (when used with other additives), and 
cement kiln dust. (CKD). Or lime kiln dust (LKD), calcium chloride, magnesium 
chloride or special products to reclaimed materials. Chemical stabilization is 
usually used when there is a need to increase the strength of reclaimed 
materials and also allow the road to be opened to traffic sooner. 
 Bituminous Stabilization: Bituminous stabilization is achieved by adding 
emulsified asphalt or foamed asphalt to the reclaimed materials. Bituminous 
stabilization is used to produce flexible reclaimed materials with better fatigue 
resistance properties than those treated with cementitious stabilization agents. 
Regardless of the type and method of stabilization or the number of stabilization agents 
used, laboratory tests must be performed to determine the amount and type of 
additives and physical properties of the reclaimed materials.

the gradation bands to reflect local conditions and construction equipment.

4 Full Depth reclamation (FDR) 
 General 
FDR is the rehabilitation technique in which the entire thickness of the asphalt 
pavement and a predetermined portion of the underlying materials (base, subbase 
and/or subgrade) is uniformly pulverized and mixed to provide a homogenous and 
improved reclaimed materials using a special equipment, Figure No. (6). New material 
is sometimes added such as emulsified asphalt or foamed asphalt, aggregate and/or 
recycling agent (if needed). The recycling process is carried out without application of 
heating. Usually, a new asphalt surface layer is laid over the produced base course.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 6: Full Depth Reclamation 
 
Often, this mixture of material, without any additional stabilizing additives, is sufficient 
to serve as the base layer for the road. However, if after proper project evaluation it is 
determined that the reclaimed materials need to be improved or modified, there are 
three different types of stabilization methods that can be used: 
 Mechanical stabilization: Mechanical stabilization is achieved by adding 
granular materials, such as new aggregate, reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) 
or crushed concrete to the reclaimed material. Mechanical stabilization is 
typically used when additional granular material is needed to supplement the 
gradation of the reclaimed material, increase the thickness of the layer to be 
treated and/or to meet the performance requirements of the mix design.

 Quality Assurance Procedures 
3.5.1 Compaction and Thickness 
Compacted density shall be determined by evaluating the achieved density relative to 
the target density established in the trial section. Individual results within 95-105% of 
target density are required. The in-place density shall be determined by the sand cone 
method according to AASHTO T191 (or ASTM D1556) or a nuclear density gauge be 
according to AASHTO T355. The thickness of the recycled layer, complete as placed and 
compacted, shall be measured from cores obtained at five (5) random locations within 
the lot. The average of the five core thicknesses shall be reported as the thickness of 
the lot 
  
3.5.2 Aggregate gradation and bitumen content 
Samples should be taken to test and evaluate the gradation of the aggregate and the 
content of the asphalt binder in the cold recycled asphalt layer as indicated in the 
specifications of the Ministry of Transport and logistic services. However, the tolerance 
limits must be adjusted from the specified job mix formula values as follows:  
 
 
  
  
The upper and lower specification limits are the approved job-mix formula target value 
(0.4 x (1 + the ratio of the reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) rounded to the nearest 
0.10%). 
Example: If 25% of the mixture is reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) material, then the 
allowable deviation from target value for percent asphalt content is 0.4 x (1.25) = 
0.5%. 
  
  
The upper and lower specification limits are the approved job-mix formula target value 
x (1 + the ratio of the reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) rounded to the nearest percent 
except for the 0.075 mm (No. 200) sieve that is rounded to the nearest 0.10%. 
Example: If 30% of the mixture (Superpave) is reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) 
material, then the allowable deviation from target value for percent of aggregate 
passing the 0.075 mm (No. 200) sieve is  1.5 x (1.30) = 2%.

3.4.1.2 Two-Unit Trains 
Consist of a large milling machine and a pugmill mix-paver. The two-unit train 
does not contain crushing and screening unit. Control of RAP maximum size and 
gradation is the same as with single unit train. Figure 5 
The advantages of the two-unit train are simplicity of operation and high 
production capacity with better control of added additives. The main 
disadvantages of this method are the limitations on RAP aggregate oversize. 
  
Figure 5: Two-unit train 
  
3.4.1.3 Multi-Unit Trains 
Multi-unit trains consist of milling machine, a crushing and screening machine, 
and a pugmill mixer for mixing additives with RAP. The main advantages of the 
multi-unit train are high productivity and high process control. The major 
disadvantage is the length of the train which can make traffic control difficult in 
urban locations 
 
3.4.1.4 Laydown, Compaction and curing 
The procedures for paving, compacting and curing of recycled layer are the same 
mentioned earlier for central plant recycling mixtures.

3.4.1 Cold in-place Recycling (CIR) 
The process of recycling is carried out on site by removing all / part of the pavement by 
milling, then mixing these materials with some additives and re-paving them. The steps 
include the following: 
• Milling of Reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) to target depth (typical 
milling depth is 50 to 100 mm) 
• Addition of recycling agent and virgin materials 
•  Mixing materials, laydown and compaction 
The recycling process is carried out without application of heat. Typically, a new 
surface asphalt layer is laid on top of the recycled layer. The recycled layer is allowed 
to dry and cure before final compaction and laying of the new asphalt surface layer. 
The basic methods and equipment that are used in the process cold in-place recycling 
include: 
3.4.1.1 Single Unit Train 
Within this method, a single unit train performs all the steps, including milling the 
existing pavement to the required depth, removing sizes greater than 1 to 1.5 
inches of RAP, adding and mixing all additives with RAP, and then paving mixed 
materials or spreading them on pavement. Single unit trains do not contain 
screening and crushing units, making control of the maximum particle size more 
difficult. Figure 4 
The advantages of the single unit train are simplicity of operation and high 
production capacity and may be preferred in urban areas. The main 
disadvantages of this method are the limitations on RAP aggregate oversize, 
limitations on precise material proportioning, and minimal mixing times.

no less than 10 tons without the use of a vibrator to hold the mixture in place, and 
then pneumatic-tired roller is used to compact the layer to its maximum density, 
but this may leave marks for tires on the surface and these marks can be removed 
by ending compaction Using the vibratory steel-wheel roller so that the density of 
the compacted layer reaches at least 96 % of the laboratory bulk density, based 
on the gyratory or Marshal compaction and must be constantly monitored during 
compaction. If cracks occur during vibratory compaction, the vibrators are turned 
off and static compaction is applied. If cracks persist, the compaction is stopped 
and the layer is allowed to cure in order to get rid of moisture, taking into account 
the start of compaction with pneumatic-tired roller followed by steel-wheel roller. 
After the completion of paving and compaction, the layer shall be allowed to cure 
for a period of not less than 3 days and not more than 10 days to reduce the 
moisture content prior to placing asphalt surface layer. It is commonly felt that if 
a dry cut core can be extracted essentially intact, then the layer has cured 
sufficiently. It is preferable not to open the recycled mix layer for traffic during 
the curing period and in the event that the layer becomes wet during rainfall, an 
additional curing time may be required. The curing process is the continued 
evaporation of water and volatile materials in the asphalt emulsion. When the 
water in the asphalt emulsion evaporates, the dispersed asphalt particles 
combine into a continuous asphalt film that covers the aggregate and when curing 
is completed, the layer has almost reached its maximum strength. After the layer 
has cured, an additional compaction is allowed prior to placing the asphalt surface 
layer.

recycling agent, water and any additives at the beginning of construction.

construction process because poor compaction can lead to premature failure or

 Construction and Production 
3.4.1 Cold Central Pavement Recycling (CCPR) 
Central plant recycling is used for projects that require high production rates or close 
control over the mix design. Central recycling is used when stockpiles of RAP is 
available or when on-site recycling is not ab option. 
3.4.1.1 Processing RAP and stockpiling 
Processing of Reclaimed Asphalt pavement (RAP) includes crushing, screening 
and storage in separate hulls. The goal is to produce homogeneous and consistent 
materials of uniform quality and with a specific range of sizes for each stock. The 
general rule is that the need for processing increases with the increase of RAP 
proportion that will be used in the recycled asphalt mix. Processed materials are 
stocked in the same manner described earlier in this manual. 
 
3.4.1.2 Equipment 
Equipment required for cold recycled mix production generally includes a RAP 
crusher, mixing and batching unit, and conventional paving equipment. The mixing 
and batching unit shall be able to produce a homogeneous recycled mixture by 
mixing RAP with asphalt emulsion, water and any other additives. 
  
  
Crushing and screening units are used to reduce the RAP size to acceptable limits to 
achieve better control over the sizing of the RAP and hence oversizing can be avoided. 
The material is then stockpiled for immediate or future use.  
  
 
The unit must have the ability to effectively mix RAP with emulsified asphalt, water and 
any other additives with each other to obtain a homogeneous mixture of cold recycled 
asphalt. Mixing shall continue until materials are fully coated and a uniform mixture is 
produced upon discharge from the mixing unit. Adjustments should be made in the

Obtaining the optimum content of emulsified asphalt can be summarized as follows: 
1. Obtain samples of RAP so that the requirements of the gradation comply with 
those specified in Table No. (2) with the addition of new aggregate if necessary. 
RAP must be dried prior to mixing at 40±2°C. 
2. Two representative RAP samples should be used to determine the average 
asphalt content and the extracted aggregate gradation according to AASHTO 
T164 and AASHTO T30. If the amount passing the 4.75 mm sieve in the 
extracted mineral aggregate gradation is greater than 65 percent or if the 
asphalt content is greater than 5.5 percent, consider adding virgin aggregates. 
3. A percentage of water ranging from 1.5 to 3% is added to the reclaimed asphalt 
pavement (RAP) and mixed well, then at least three different proportions of 
emulsified asphalt are added and mixed. Mixing should be done at ambient 
temperature (25±5°C) so that water is added first and mixed, then the 
emulsified asphalt is added and mixing continues. One sample should be mixed 
at a time and the mixing time should not exceed 60 seconds. 
4. After mixing, samples are cured in plastic containers at a temperature of 
40±2°C for 30±3 minutes. After curing samples are compacted either by using 
a gyratory compactor at a number of gyrations equal to 30 to produce a sample 
with a height of 95 ± 5 mm and a diameter of 150 mm or by using a Marshall 
hammer and a number of blows equal to 75 per side to produce a sample with 
a height of 63.5 ± 2.5 mm and a diameter of 100 mm. Ensure all materials pass 
the 25mm sieve for 100mm diameter specimens. A total of 6 samples should 
be prepared for each content of emulsified asphalt for the indirect tensile 
strength test or the Marshall stability test and divided into two sets, three of 
these samples are called the dry set and placed in the air, while the other three 
samples are called the wet set and placed in a bath Water at a certain 
temperature for a certain period of time. Average percentage of air voids for 
each group should be approximately equal. 
5. After compaction, remove the samples from the mold and place them in an 
oven equipped with ventilation outlets for curing at a temperature of 60 ± 1 °C 
to obtain a constant sample weight for a period of not less than 24 hours and 
not more than 48 hours. The constant weight of a sample is defined as 0.05%

Asphalt emulsion must be selected to meet standards and specifications and to achieve 
proper mixing with RAP. The choice of the appropriate type of bitumen emulsion 
(cationic or anionic) with or without polymers is determined by the type of aggregate, 
whether it is siliceous or calcareous. Cationic emulsion is suitable for siliceous 
aggregate (e.g., granite), and anionic emulsion is suitable for calcareous aggregate 
(e.g., limestone). This is important because it severely affects the adhesion of the base 
asphalt binder in the emulsion to the surface of the aggregate. Medium to slow-setting 
emulsions are suitable for hot tropical conditions so as to guard against setting before 
compaction is carried out. 
The process of designing using emulsified asphalt is based on determining the optimal 
content to cover the RAP and new aggregates to obtain a homogeneous mixture of 
suitable strength that meets the requirements. The optimum emulsion content is 
defined as the minimum content to meet the mixture requirements shown in Table (3).

After the required samples are obtained, the gradation of RAP is determined according 
to AASHTO T27. The asphalt binder is extracted from RAP to determine its quantity, 
gradation and properties of the aggregate by dissolving RAP in a standard solution to 
separate its components. After the extraction process is completed, the asphalt binder 
is recovered from the solvent by the "AASHTO R59" method, and its physical properties 
are determined. 
 
  
Normally, no new aggregate is added to the in-place hot mix recycled asphalt. The 
addition of new aggregate is not only based on the gradation of the RAP aggregate but 
is also added when there is an increase of binder content in the RAP or when it is 
desired to increase the structural capacity of the mixture and improve its volumetric 
properties. The new aggregate must be selected so that the mix of the new aggregate 
and the RAP aggregate meets the gradation requirements specified in Table No. (2) 
  
  
  
The choice of recycling agent depends on the performance grade or viscosity of the 
asphalt binder in the RAP. The effectiveness of recycling agents in restoring the 
properties of the aged asphalt binder depends on a number of factors. The interactions 
between the recycling agent and the aging asphalt binder are complex and depend on 
the time and temperature-dependent interaction between the recycling agent and the 
aged binder and these interactions are currently poorly understood. Therefore, when 
determining the amount and type of recycling agent, it is recommended to test the 
mechanical properties of the recycled mixture, rather than simply relying on blending 
charts.

 Design of Cold Recycled Asphalt Mix 
3.3.1 Design steps 
Although there is no globally accepted design guide for cold mix asphalt recycling, 
guidelines have been developed by several road agencies based on laboratory tests, 
empirical formulas, or previous experience on similar projects. The design steps can 
be summarized as follows: 
1. Obtaining samples of reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) from stockpiles 
according to AASHTO R 90 specifications or take core samples from the 
existing pavement. 
2. Extraction and recovery of asphalt binder from RAP to determine its 
percentage, physical properties and gradation of RAP. 
3. Choosing the gradation and quantity of new aggregate added to the recycled 
mix (if necessary). 
4. Determining the type of virgin asphalt binder (emulsion asphalt/foam asphalt) 
and/or the recycling agent required to restore the properties of the aged 
asphalt binder. 
5. Determining the optimum content of emulsified asphalt or foamed asphalt to 
achieve the design criteria of the mixture such as indirect tensile strength 
(superpave) or Marshall stability. 
6. Determining the job mix formula (JMF). Once all laboratory tests of the 
materials are completed, the information is available to create a job Mix 
Formula (JMF) 
 
  
  
Representative samples of the existing road layer should be obtained and evaluated to 
properly design the cold mix recycled asphalt. Samples are traditionally obtained by 
taking slabs or core samples with a diameter of 150 mm through the entire depth of the 
layer to be recycled, and then crushing those samples in the laboratory to prepare them 
for the rest of the tests.

3.2.7 Chemical Additives (Optional) 
There are several types of mineral or chemical additives that have proven useful 
in creating pavements from recycled asphalt. Anti-stripping additives are 
effective in reducing sensitivity to moisture. Dry materials such as fly ash, 
Portland cement and lime, which is usually used in the form of a slurry to reduce 
dust and to speed up the curing process, as well as to gain strength for the 
recycled layer in a faster time. For example, Portland cement absorbs the water 
in the asphalt emulsion, which leads to better stability and faster curing. The use 
of these material also reduces the occurrence of early rutting during the curing 
period, especially for mixtures with a fine gradation or that contain aggregates 
with round grains.  
• cement 
Cement may be added as specified in the mix design and to comply with 
specifications for type 1 or type 2 cement (AASHTO M 85, ASHTO M 240 or ASTM 
C150, ASTM C595) and the ratio of asphalt to dry cement must be at least 3:1. In 
addition, the proportion of cement should not exceed 1% of the dry weight of 
reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) and the contractor must provide details of 
cement mixing process during the recycling process for approval.

3.2.3 Emulsified Asphalt 
Emulsified asphalt is the most common material for use with cold recycled 
asphalt mixtures. emulsified asphalt should be selected to meet standards and 
specifications and to achieve proper mixing with reclaimed asphalt pavement 
(RAP). For cationic emulsified asphalt, use CSS-1 or CSS-1h conforming to 
AASHTO M208. For anionic emulsified asphalt, use HFMS-2, HFMS-2h, or HFM S-
2s conforming to AASHTO M140 
3.2.4 Foamed Asphalt 
Foamed asphalt is produced by mixing hot asphalt binder with small amounts of 
cold water. When cold water comes into contact with hot bitumen, it turns into 
steam, which becomes trapped in the form of bubbles on the asphalt. The result 
is that the hot asphalt binder expands and turns into a thin layer asphalt foam that 
has a high covering capacity that is about 10 times more than that of a 
conventional asphalt binder. The foam condition lasts only for a few minutes, after 
which the asphalt binder returns to its original properties and this system 
requires a special chamber to form the foamed asphalt. The foamy state of the 
bitumen allows it to disperse and cover the reclaimed asphalt pavement 
aggregate well. 
  
3.2.5 Recycling Agent 
The most common recycling agents used in the cold recycling process are the 
emulsified recycling agents. The recycling agent must comply with ASTM D5505. 
  
  
3.2.6 Water 
Water can be added to reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) to achieve a 
homogeneous easily compacted mixture. Water should be free of any high 
concentrations of acids, alkalis, salts, sugars and other organic materials, 
chemicals or harmful substances and if the water quality is questionable, it should

3.2.2 Aggregate 
Although it is not often necessary to add new aggregates, some projects may 
require adding new aggregates in order to adjust the gradation, improve the 
stability and durability, or reduce the average content of the asphalt binder (due 
to bleeding, for example). The new aggregate could be from the same source as 
the reclaimed asphalt pavement aggregate or a different source. The new 
aggregate must be evaluated before use to ensure that it conforms to the 
specifications in terms of gradation and physical properties.

3   Cold Asphalt pavement Recycling  
 General 
In general, cold asphalt recycling is defined as a mixture of reclaimed asphalt pavement 
(RAP) with new materials such as asphalt emulsifier or foamed asphalt, aggregate 
and/or recycling agent (if needed) to produce cold base mixtures. The mixing is carried 
out in a central mixing unit or on-site using equipment Especially for that. The recycling 
process is carried out without application of heating. Usually, a new asphalt surface 
layer is laid over the produced cold base course while allowing it to dry and cure before 
final compaction and laying the new asphalt surface layer. Cold recycled asphalt 
mixture is often used on high-traffic roads as a type of repair and maintenance with a 
new asphalt surface layer on top of the produced cold base course and can also be used 
on low-traffic or secondary roads without the need for a new asphalt surface layer. 
 
 Materials 
3.2.1 Reclaimed asphalt Pavement (RAP) 
Milled reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) shall consist of aggregates coated with 
asphalt only and shall be free of dirt, soil layer materials, concrete or other 
harmful materials such as silt and mud. Samples of reclaimed asphalt pavement 
(RAP) are obtained from the stockpiles or by taking core samples. Reclaimed 
asphalt pavement (RAP) shall meet the gradation requirements specified in Table 
No. (2). It is recommended to adjust the gradation bands to reflect local conditions 
and construction equipment. 
 The properties of reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) are determined by detailed 
laboratory testing. Tests on material from obtained samples should include: 
• Moisture content of reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP). 
• Percentage and physical properties of recovered asphalt binder  
• Properties of reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) aggregate, including 
gradation and angular characteristics. 
• Maximum Specific gravity of reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) 
Reclaimed Asphalt Pavement (RAP)

the use of softer bitumen and/or recycling agent must be balanced in order to 
reduce the hardness of the final mix without over-softening the produced mixture 
binder, which may increase the potential for rutting.

content of the asphalt binder in the hot recycled asphalt layer as indicated in the

2.5.1.2 Existing Production Techniques  
Reclaimed Asphalt Pavement (RAP) cannot be added to the mixing unit as 
aggregate is added because excessive blue smoke will be produced due to the 
combustion of the old asphalt binder. Most conventional production techniques 
limit the maximum amount of RAP that can be added to the mix. RAP usually 
contains a percentage of moisture which leads to a decrease in the production 
rate because it requires additional mixing time to dry and to heat sufficiently, and 
steam is usually produced during contact between the very hot aggregate and the 
cold RAP due to the evaporation of moisture. For these reasons, it is always 
recommended to keep RAP stockpiles as dry as possible. 
Some modifications to conventional mixing plants allow the addition of 30-35% of 
RAP and in order to add higher percentage with the reduce of the above problems, 
it is necessary to heat the RAP. Direct contact between RAP and heating flames in 
the mixing plant should be avoided as this leads to further aging of RAP and 
release of hydrocarbon emissions (blue smoke). On the other hand, proper 
preheating of RAP allows it to soften, decompose and dry. Technical problems 
related to the quantity limitations of RAP can be overcome by developing and/or 
modifying asphalt mixing plants. However, additional costs resulting from the 
process of upgrading or modifying plants should be evaluated. 
 
2.5.1.3 Properties of the produced mix  
From a technical point of view, the increase of RAP amount leads to an increase 
in the hardness of the produced mix, which depends mainly on the hardness of 
the asphalt binder in RAP. Usually, the higher hardness asphalt mixture is less 
resistant to fatigue cracks or thermal and reflective cracks, which is the main 
reason for the reluctance of official authorities to use RAP. Several studies have 
shown that the presence of aged asphalt binder in a well-designed mixture 
reduces moisture sensitivity and permanent deformations such as rutting. 
However, many factors may adversely affect the moisture sensitivity of the 
mixture, such as insufficient mixing of the aged asphalt binder with the virgin 
Existing Production Techniques 
Properties of the produced mix

 Challenges 
2.5.1 Limitation on high percentage of RAP 
2.5.1.1 Quality and homogeneity of RAP aggregate 
In order to consider RAP as a controlled element during the design of the mixture, 
the quality and homogeneity of the RAP aggregate must be evaluated. In general, 
RAP has a high content of fine materials due to the milling operations of the 
pavement layers and/or the crushing operations to reduce the size of the 
aggregate. The amount of fine material may limit the maximum content of RAP 
added to the mix because the grading requirements of the final mix may not be 
met. Moreover, fine materials contain more amount of aged asphalt binder and 
fine materials also tend to retain moisture. All these aspects must be taken into 
consideration to properly manage the use of RAP when designing and producing 
the mixture. 
The materials obtained through the milling process from a single source, are 
usually very homogeneous materials and sometimes no further crushing and/or 
screening operations are required. However, the quality of materials removed 
from the pavement layers depends on the milling machine, its speed, and the 
depth of milling. The depth of milling is an important factor because a single-layer 
material has homogeneous properties (in terms of gradation, properties of the 
asphalt binder and its content). Good milling process ensures high quality of the 
removed material. Storing RAP from specific sources (specified projects) in 
separate stockpiles and making sure that the removed material is not 
contaminated with soil materials increases the uniformity of the RAP. However, it 
is often impractical for asphalt mix producers to store materials from different 
sources in separate stockpiles due to the limited storage space available in 
asphalt plants. In these cases, additional processes must be carried out to 
homogenize the materials before they can be used in the mixture. 
Quality and homogeneity of RAP

2.4.2.3 Remixing 
The remixing method is used when additional aggregate or even new hot asphalt 
mix is required to improve the gradation or increase the thickness of the recycled 
layer. It mainly consists of: 
• Drying and heating the top layer of the existing pavement. 
• Scarifying the heated asphalt layer. 
• Adding virgin aggregate, recycling agent and/or hot asphalt mix as 
required in the design of the mixture. 
• Mixing materials together and laying of the recycled mix as a single, 
homogeneous mix. 
• Compaction of recycled materials using conventional HMA rollers and 
procedures. 
The re-mixing process is carried out using special units known together as the 
HIR train, which usually consists of: 
• One or more units for heating the existing layer. Each heating unit may 
contain scarifying teeth. 
• Mixing unit in which materials are mixed together. 
• Paving unit 
Remixing methods are categorized into multi-stage or single-stage. In the single-
stage method, the old paving layer is heated and then scarified to the full required 
depth at one time. In the multi-stage method, each unit removes a certain depth 
of the layer to be treated. For example, the first unit heats and removes 25 mm of 
the layer, then the next unit heats and removes another 25 mm, thus reaching a 
depth of 75 mm. 
Remixing

road can then be opened to traffic. In some low-traffic roads, the recycled layer can be 
left as a surface layer however it is more common that an asphalt surface layer is added 
in what is known as the Multiple Pass Operations, otherwise it is known as the Single 
Pass Operation. 
2.4.2.2 Repaving  
Repaving is a process in which the existing pavement is heated and scarified or 
milled to a depth of 50mm and a rejuvenating agent is added and then paving 
these materials immediately followed by paving the hot asphalt surface layer 
forming a good bond between the recycled layer and the new layer. These two 
operations can be completed using special units known together as the HIR train. 
The methods of repaving are classified into Multiple Pass Operations and Single 
Pass Operation. In the single-pass process, the last unit in the HIR train places the 
conventional hot mix asphalt on the surface of the screeded but uncompacted 
recycled layer, and then the traditional roller is introduced to compact the two 
layers together at the same time. In the multi-pass process, the last unit mixes 
and places the recycled materials and a separate paver then follows immediately 
behind and places the conventional hot mix asphalt on the uncompacted recycled 
mix. Both the recycled mix and the new overlay material are then compacted as 
one thick lift.  
 The current practice when repaving is to preheat the existing pavement surface to 
about 190°C using one or more heating units and then remove the layer through 
Scarifying to a depth of about 25 mm. The Scarifying teeth can be controlled to reach a 
greater depth. The amount of rejuvenating agent to be added is determined in the 
laboratory and then applied in the field by spraying directly on the road or directly into 
the mixing chamber. After placing the recycled materials, the new asphalt surface layer 
is directly placed and the aim is to create a good bond of the new asphalt surface layer 
to the surface of the recycled layer. This is easily achieved if the recycled layer can be 
kept hot at a temperature of about 100°C. The repaving method has been proven to be 
a practical way to maintain aged asphalt layers. 
Repaving

• Drying and heating the top layer of the existing pavement. 
• Scarifying the heated asphalt layer. 
• Adding a recycling agent based on the mix design. 
• Mixing recycled materials and then paving the recycled mix. 
• Compacting the recycled mix using conventional HMA rollers and 
procedures 
 Virgin aggregate is not added during the surface recycling process, so the modification 
of the existing asphalt layer is limited only to the rejuvenation of the aged asphalt 
binder. 
The old layer is heated using propane gas infrared heaters and has proven to be better 
than direct flame heaters. One or more heating units are used in tandem to gradually 
raise the surface temperature of the pavement until it reaches 150°C so that the 
scarifying teeth can peel or scratch the surface of the layer. The temperature that can 
be reached depends on the capacity of the heating unit, the temperature of the surface 
and the atmosphere, and the duration of exposure of the surface to heating. Scarifying 
teeth have the ability to peel the layer to a depth of 50 mm, but usually the layer is 
scarified to a depth of 25 mm. Recycling agent is added, if necessary, to the hot 
scratched asphalt layer by an automated system which is related to the running speed 
of the equipment. Recycling agent utilization rates vary, depending on the condition of 
the asphalt binder in the old asphalt, the type of recycler used, and the design 
requirements of the recycled mix. The recycling agent can also be heated before adding 
to the highest temperature recommended by the recycling agent manufacturer. Once 
the recycling agent has been added to the scarified layer, mixing is carried out using a 
stirring auger to obtain a homogeneous mixture. Sometimes the recycling agent is 
added to the surface of the layer prior to being scarified, as this procedure allows the 
recycling agent to be mixed with the materials of the old asphalt layer during the 
passage of the scarifying teeth. After the mixing is completed, the materials are paved 
and compacted using conventional asphalt pavement equipment. Once compaction is 
completed and the layer is allowed to cool, the recycling process is complete and the

2.4.2 Hot in-place Recycling (HIR) 
The process of hot in-place recycling is provided by removing part of the pavement 
surface by scarification after applying heat to soften the asphalt surface and then 
mixing these materials as necessary with the recycling agent, new aggregate or an 
additional new asphalt mix and then re-paving. Hot in-place recycling is considered 
effective to correct surface distresses not caused by structural inadequacy, such as 
rutting, thermal cracks, shoving, raveling and others, provided that the depth of these 
deformations does not exceed 50 mm. It should be noted that the Hot in-place recycling 
process is used only if the layers under the asphalt surface are structurally in good 
condition. The recycling process mainly consists of four steps: 
• Softening the surface of asphalt pavement with heat. 
• Scarification and/or mechanical removal of surface material (usually from 
20 to 50 mm). 
• Mixing surface materials with recycling agent and/or new aggregate and 
virgin asphalt binder. 
• Placing and paving the recycled mix on the pavement surface. 
There are three basic types that are commonly used in hot in-place recycling process: 
 
2.4.2.1 Surface Recycling 
A simple process in which the surface of the existing pavement is heated and 
scarified with a set of scarifying teeth or tines to a depth of about 25 mm and 
mixed with a recycling agent, then leveled and compacted. Virgin aggregate is not 
usually added to the material, a new wearing course was often added later. 
Surface recycling (also known as heater scarification) is the earliest form of HIR 
and was widely used in the 1950s, 1960s and early 1970s. Compared to other 
forms of HIR, surface recycling is the least technologically complex method and it 
mainly consists of: 
 
Surface Recycling

It is possible to increase the RAP added to the mixt up to 70% if it is heated to a 
temperature lower than the virgin aggregate before being introduced to the mixing 
chamber using a separate dryer. This procedure requires modification of the mixing 
unit and RAP must be transferred to a separate hot bin with its own weighing hopper. 
The disadvantages of this method include the very high initial investment. Moreover, 
super heating of the aggregate, though to temperatures lower than what was 
previously mentioned, is still required. 
  
  
Reclaimed asphalt pavement RAP is discharged into the hot elevator at the point where 
the hot aggregate is discharged from the drying drum. The RAP mixes with the hot 
aggregate in the aggregate elevator, which leads to the release of steam as a result of 
the gradual evaporation of moisture from RAP as the materials are lifted in the elevator 
to the sieving surface to be sieved and stored in the hot bins. This process facilitates a 
good transfer of heat from the hot aggregate to RAP as it remains in contact with the 
hot aggregate for a relatively longer time and this eliminates the formation of thick 
steams in the mixing chamber. In the case of sieving materials in the mixer sieves, this 
procedure limits the maximum amount RAP to relatively low proportions (10-15%) to 
avoid the asphalt binder surrounding the RAP aggregate clogging the sieve openings. 
The amount of RAP can be increased by up to 40% by transferring the material from the 
heated elevator directly to an additional hot bin without passing through the sieves but 
the drawback of this procedure is less control of the RAP gradation. Figure 3

Using a RAP bin, the RAP is delivered by belt conveyor directly to the weighing hopper

RAP is placed between the hot virgin aggregate and gets more time to heat up. The 
aggregate must be superheated to a certain temperature to transfer heat and dry the 
cold RAP. A slight adjustment to the mixing time may be required to allow for better 
heat transfer. It must be noted that when discharging RAP with the very hot aggregate 
in the mixing chamber, water vapor will be emitted due to the evaporation of moisture 
from RAP. Therefore, it is necessary to ensure that the steam generated during the first 
few seconds is properly ventilated in the dust extraction system. Figure 2

• Covering RAP with plastic sheets should be avoided because it causes 
water condensation which increases the moisture content. The exception 
to this is when rain is imminent where the stockpiles can be temporarily 
covered to prevent the material from getting wet and once the rain stops 
the stockpiles must be uncovered again to reduce the effect of water 
condensation. 
• The best measure to prevent moisture build-up in RAP is to store it under 
an open-sided roof which will allow moisture to evaporate while protecting 
the RAP from rain. 
 
2.4.1.3 Adding RAP to Asphalt Plant Mixer 
Some modifications to the conventional asphalt mixing plants are required in 
order to be able to produce recycled mixtures of the same quality as the mixtures 
that use aggregates and raw asphalt binder. There are two main types of asphalt 
mixing plants, the first type known as batch production mixers and the second 
type known as continuous production mixers. The first type is the most widely 
used in the Kingdom, so we will focus on it. 
Whatever the configuration of the mixing plant, direct contact between RAP and 
flame should be avoided. Also, plant productivity must be adjusted and mixing 
time adjusted to allow heat to be transferred to RAP and heated to a temperature 
that enables proper mixing of the materials with each other. 
There are many ways to add RAP to the asphalt plant mixer, including the 
following:

2.4.1.1 Processing of RAP 
Processing of Reclaimed Asphalt pavement (RAP) includes crushing, screening 
and stockpiling in separate hulls. The goal is to produce homogeneous and 
consistent materials of uniform quality and with a specific range of sizes for each 
stock. The general rule is that the need for processing increases with the increase 
of RAP proportion that will be used in the recycled asphalt mix. It is common 
practice to crush RAP to a size equal to or less than one sieve size than the 
nominal maximum size of the mix. RAP processing step is an important step and 
should not be neglected, especially when asphalt mixtures are produced with high 
percentages of RAP above 15%, it is necessary to screen and fractionate RAP to 
ensure a homogeneous and consistent product that will not impair the quality of 
the recycled asphalt mix. 
However, in the case of recycled asphalt mixtures with low RAP content of less 
than 15% it is usually only necessary to require a screening and crushing unit to 
control the maximum size of the RAP or a scalping screen may be used to remove 
oversize RAP 
 
2.4.1.2 Stockpiling processed RAP 
Segregation, tending to recompact and moisture in RAP are among the most 
important problems related to the stockpiling of RAP. The following factors must 
be taken into account when stockpiling RAP: 
• The surface on which the RAP is stored must be inclined (ideally six 
degrees) to allow water to drain. 
• The surface on which the RAP is stored must be of a reinforced type 
(concrete for example) so that the materials are not contaminated with 
groundwater or soil. 
• Where possible, processed RAP should be stocked in conical stockpiles 
with low-slope sides. A crust will form on the surface of the stockpile, 
Processing of RAP 
Stockpiling Processed RAP

 Construction and Production 
2.4.1 Hot Central Asphalt Plant Recycling (HCPR) 
Most of reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia is obtained 
through the cold milling process, rather than crushing the asphalt with excavators or 
bulldozers. The surplus asphalt that was stored at the site of the asphalt mixing plant 
can also be used. The main advantages of using asphalt milling machine instead of 
crushing are that they: 
• It works to break up the asphalt layers to obtain a product with a fairly 
uniform classification. 
• Capable of removing asphalt layers precisely to the specified thickness, 
allowing to obtain a homogeneous material where this is desirable during 
the design phase, especially if the pavement to be removed consists of 
layers of different classification. 
• Reduces the risk of contaminating RAP with materials from non-asphalt 
layers. 
After obtaining RAP, it is stockpiled at the site of the asphalt plant and in general the 
usual practices followed during aggregate stockpiling should be followed. RAP from 
different sources containing different contents of asphalt binder and different 
aggregate gradations should be stored separately, to ensure the homogeneity of the 
materials being added to the hot mix asphalt. 
RAP later goes through the stage of processing, stockpiling and then adding to the 
asphalt plant mixer, and the details are as follows.

After extraction and recovery of asphalt binder from old RAP, its physical properties 
such as viscosity, penetration and performance grade (PG) are determined. These 
properties are used in the blending charts according to ASTM D4887 to help determine 
the quantity and type of virgin asphalt binder and/or a recycling agent added to the 
mixture. Appendix (A) 
The use of a blending chart usually provides a reasonable but slightly high estimate of 
the amount of recycling agent required so the correct amount must be ensured by 
necessary laboratory tests and adjustments are made if necessary. The recycling agent 
must comply with ASTM D4552. 
  
  
  
Samples of the recycled asphalt mixture are prepared, compacted and tested according 
to the traditional methods used during the design of hot mix asphalt (HMA). After 
completing the standard tests, the results are evaluated to ensure that the properties 
of the recycled mix asphalt conform to the specifications of the Ministry of Transport 
and logistic services.

Representative samples of the existing road layer should be obtained and evaluated to 
properly design the in-place hot mix recycled asphalt. Samples are traditionally 
obtained by taking slabs or core samples with a diameter of 150 mm through the entire 
depth of the layer to be recycled, and then crushing those samples in the laboratory to 
prepare them for the rest of the tests. 
  
 
After the required samples are obtained, the gradation of RAP is determined according 
to AASHTO T27. The asphalt binder is extracted from RAP to determine its quantity, 
gradation and properties of the aggregate by dissolving RAP in a standard solution to 
separate its components. After the extraction process is completed, the asphalt binder 
is recovered from the solvent by the "AASHTO R59" method, and its physical properties 
are determined. 
  
 
Normally, no new aggregate is added to the in-place hot mix recycled asphalt. The 
addition of new aggregate is not only based on the gradation of the RAP aggregate but 
is also added when there is an increase of binder content in the RAP or when it is 
desired to increase the structural capacity of the mixture and improve its volumetric 
properties. The new aggregate must be selected so that the mix of the new aggregate 
and the RAP aggregate meets the standard specifications of the Ministry for both 
gradation and quality.

2.3.2 Hot in-place Recycling (HIR) 
As described earlier, the objective of designing a hot mix recycled asphalt in place is to 
restore the properties of the aged asphalt pavement and to obtain a recycled layer with 
the same properties as the new layer or at least as close to it as possible. It should be 
noted that the amount of RAP used in place to produce recycled asphalt mixtures 
usually ranges from 80 to 100%, which is higher than that used in central plant 
recycling. 
  
2.3.2.1 Design Steps 
The following is a summary of the main steps involved when designing hot mix recycled 
asphalt in place. 
• Withdrawing a sufficient number of samples from the existing road to be 
recycled using standard methods. 
• Extraction and recovery of asphalt binder from RAP to determine its 
percentage, physical properties and gradation of RAP. 
• Choosing the gradation and quantity of new aggregate added to the recycled 
mix (if necessary). 
• Using of blending scheme to determine the amount and type of virgin asphalt 
binder and/or recycling agent required to regenerate the properties of the 
aged asphalt binder. Appendix (A) 
• Preparing specimens of the mixture and test in the laboratory. 
• Determining the job mix formula (JMF). Once all laboratory tests of the 
materials are completed, the information is available to create a job Mix 
Formula (JMF)

2.3.1.2 Marshall System 
A. Design steps for Marshall System 
The process of designing HMA using RAP is the same as the virgin HMA design with the 
following additional guidelines regarding the addition of RAP. Sampling of both 
Aggregate and RAP from stockpile are similar. Obtain representative RAP samples 
according to AASHTO R 90. RAP binder content and aggregate characterization and RAP 
moisture are as follow: 
  
  
Produced HMA total binder content is consisting of virgin binder and RAP binder 
contained in the RAP. It is important to know how much asphalt binder is present in the 
RAP material, so that it can be accounted for in the mix design process. Determine 
binder content of each RAP sample using extraction method. Sometimes, it is also 
necessary to know something about the physical properties of the asphalt binder. In 
these cases, it is necessary to extract and recover the asphalt binder from the RAP 
using a solvent so that the binder can be tested. 
 For low RAP contents less than 15 percent, it is not necessary to do this testing 
because there is not enough of the RAP binder present to change the total binder 
properties. 
 For intermediate ranges of RAP 15 to 25 percent, the virgin binder grade can 
simply be dropped one grade. 
 For higher percentages of RAP, you will need to extract and recover the RAP 
binder and determine its properties (Appendix A) 
  
  
RAP aggregate must meet the property requirements like virgin aggregate. The RAP 
aggregate must be analyzed to determine his gradation, specific gravity and consensus 
properties. It is best to separate the reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) into coarse 
fraction retained on sieve No. 4 (4.75 mm) and fine fraction passing sieve No. 4 (4.75 
mm) and then determine aggregate properties and gradation.

 Design of Recycled Asphalt Mix 
2.3.1 Hot Central Asphalt Plant Recycling (HCPR) 
Old paving materials usually contain a solid asphalt bond due to oxidation and also 
contain fragmented aggregate (an increase in fine materials) due to scraping or 
crushing layers. This usually requires softening the asphalt binder to achieve the 
targeted performance grade and selecting the new aggregate gradient and its 
properties to achieve the target gradient and the desired quality standards. It is also 
necessary to add a quantity of the new asphalt binder sufficient to cover the added 
aggregate and softening (modifying the properties) of the old asphalt binder. It is 
important to know how much asphalt binder is present in the RAP material, so that it 
can be accounted for in the mix design process. Sometimes, it is also necessary to know 
some physical properties of the old asphalt binder. 
This is achieved in the following way: 
 Withdrawing a sufficient number of random samples from stockpiles or from the 
existing road to be recycled using standard methods. 
 Extraction of on the RAP to determine the asphalt binder content and properties 
of the aggregate by dissolving it in a standard solution to separate its 
components. 
 Recovery of the old asphalt binder from the solvent according to Abson method 
(AASHTO R 59) and determining its psychical properties. 
The overall process remains the same as for the virgin HMA design either for 
Superpave or Marshall mix design; however, extra procedures are required to 
accommodate the use of RAP. The differences include the following: 
a. Change in virgin binder grade may be needed depending on the amount of RAP, 
desired final binder grade, and RAP binder stiffness. 
b. The RAP aggregate is treated like another stockpile for blending and weighing. 
c. The weight of the binder in the RAP must be accounted for when batching 
aggregates. 
d. The RAP aggregate specific gravity must be estimated. 
e. The total asphalt content is reduced to compensate for the binder provided by 
the RAP. 
.1
3.
2
  Hot Central Asphalt Plant Recycling (HCPR) 
2. Hot Asphalt Pavement Recycling 
  
  
 
  
Recycling Asphalt Pavement 
within Road Projects 
Kingdom of Saudi Arabia 
Roads general Authority 
General Department of Quality 
& Environment
 صفحة 16 
 من 77
 
2.2.2 Asphalt binder & Recycling agent 
Recycling agent is defined as an organic material with selected chemical and 
physical properties to restore reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) to the desired 
specifications. Usually reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) contain a solid asphalt 
bond due to oxidation and this requires softening the asphalt binder to achieve 
the targeted viscosity or performance grade. The purpose of adding raw asphalt 
binder and/or recycling agents is to restore the properties of the aging asphalt 
binder and to provide sufficient additional binder to cover the reclaimed asphalt 
pavement (RAP) and any new aggregate added. The properties of the aged 
asphalt binder can be restored either by adding raw asphalt binder or a recycling 
agent separately or by adding them together. The choice of the appropriate 
method depends on the properties of the aged binder and its percentage in the 
reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) and the percentage of reclaimed asphalt 
pavement (RAP) in the recycled mixture. 
  
2.2.3 New Aggregate 
Although in practice it is of little use, it can be added in order to adjust the 
gradation and improve the stiffness of the recycled mix, as well as possibly 
correcting the percentage of excess asphalt binder. The new aggregate may be 
from the same or different source than the reclaimed asphalt pavement 
aggregate, but it will need to meet all material specifications especially the 
gradation to meet the final gradation of the mixed materials together.

2.2.1  Reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) 
Reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) shall consist of aggregates coated with 
asphalt only and shall be free of dirt, base layer materials, concrete or other 
harmful materials such as silt and mud. Samples of reclaimed asphalt pavement 
(RAP) are obtained by sampling core samples or taking random samples from 
stockpiles. Gradation limits for reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) should be as 
shown in Table (1) 
Table (1) Reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) gradation limits 
Percent Passing 
Standard Sieve 
100 
37.5 (1.5) 
90 – 100 
25 (1) 
 
The properties of reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) are determined by detailed 
laboratory testing. Tests on material from obtained samples should include: 
 Moisture content of reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP). 
 Percentage and physical properties of asphalt binder recovered from the 
reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP). 
 Properties of reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) aggregate, including 
gradation and angular characteristics. 
 Maximum Specific gravity of reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) 
 Volumetric properties such as air voids (AV), voids in mineral aggregate (VMA) 
and voids filled with asphalt (VFA). 
This information provides a starting point for the engineer to assess the current 
condition of the existing pavement and use it to help design the mixture to achieve the 
desired properties.

2  Hot Asphalt Pavement Recycling 
 General 
In general, hot recycled asphalt is defined as the process in which reclaimed asphalt 
pavement (RAP) materials are combined with new materials such as asphalt binder, 
aggregate and/or recycling agent (as needed). Mixing is done in a central mixing plant 
or on site using Special equipment for that. Hot mix asphalt recycling uses a method 
of heat transfer to the existing pavement to soften it and facilitate its mixing with raw 
materials such as aggregate, asphalt binder and/or recycling agent and this method 
is currently the most widely used method of recycling existing pavement worldwide. 
Reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) is obtained by milling, and ripping and crushing 
existing pavement. Thanks to the use of reclaimed asphalt pavement (RAP) in the 
production of recycled asphalt, the United States saved 4.1 million tons of asphalt 
binder and 78 million tons of aggregate in 2018. 
The most important factors that make pavement recycling interesting are cost 
reduction, raw material conservation, and environmental considerations.

 Cold In-place Recycling (CIR) 
This method includes reusing the asphalt pavement with the addition of Virgin 
aggregate or recycling agent or both and mixing in place to obtain a suitable asphalt 
base course on which new asphalt layers are laid, or reusing the entire layers of 
asphalt pavement with the underlying gravel/earth layers (Full Depth Reclamation, 
FDR) to produce a base course with the addition of additives, including asphalt 
emulsions, chemicals, calcium chloride, Portland cement, lime, etc., and new 
materials (if necessary) to obtain the required thicknesses and design levels. This 
method often includes several steps, including: milling and crushing works of the 
existing pavement, adding new materials and mixing, paving according to the design 
road sector, compaction and finally placement of new asphalt surface course. Reusing 
of the existing pavement includes many advantages, lower costs, the possibility of 
modifying the road sector and adjusting its engineering design, eliminating the 
reflective cracks that may appear in layers of new asphalt pavements, and there are 
many challenges to this method, including: Eliminate reflective cracks that may 
appear in layers of new asphalt pavements, and there are many challenges to this 
method, including: 
 Contractors need to purchase new equipment (the price of this equipment is 
about 5 million riyals). The equipment includes special milling and mixing units, 
a crushing unit, storage and feeding additives units. 
 Not suitable if there are many facilities within the layers of the road to be 
reused. 
 Not suitable for existing pavement that includes geotextile/rubber/crack 
fillers. 
 Difficulty controlling the quality and regularity of the recycled layer. 
 A treatment period of 2-15 days is required for the resurfacing layer specially 
with the use of asphalt emulsions. 
Figure 1 shows the various methods of recycling existing pavement

 Cold In-place Recycling (CIR)
